</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">Organic compounds
</td>
<td style="text-align:center;"> Organic compounds (chemoheterotrophs) or carbon fixation (chemoautotrophs)  
</td>
<td> <i><a href="/wiki/Bacillus" title="Bacillus">Bacillus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Clostridium" title="Clostridium">Clostridium</a></i>, or <a href="/wiki/Enterobacteriaceae" title="Enterobacteriaceae">Enterobacteriaceae</a> 
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In many ways, bacterial metabolism provides traits that are useful for ecological stability and for human society. One example is that some bacteria called <a href="/wiki/Diazotroph" title="Diazotroph">diazotrophs</a> have the ability to <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation" title="Nitrogen fixation">fix nitrogen</a> gas using the enzyme <a href="/wiki/Nitrogenase" title="Nitrogenase">nitrogenase</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid34108945_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid34108945-105">[105]</a></sup> This environmentally important trait can be found in bacteria of most metabolic types listed above.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> This leads to the ecologically important processes of <a href="/wiki/Denitrification" title="Denitrification">denitrification</a>, sulfate reduction, and <a href="/wiki/Acetogenesis" title="Acetogenesis">acetogenesis</a>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32559887_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32559887-107">[107]</a></sup> Bacterial metabolic processes are also important in biological responses to <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a>; for example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulfate-reducing_bacteria" title="Sulfate-reducing bacteria">sulfate-reducing bacteria</a> are largely responsible for the production of the highly toxic forms of <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> (<a href="/wiki/Methylmercury" title="Methylmercury">methyl-</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dimethylmercury" title="Dimethylmercury">dimethylmercury</a>) in the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Non-respiratory anaerobes use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">fermentation</a> to generate energy and reducing power, secreting metabolic by-products (such as <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> in brewing) as waste. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Facultative_anaerobe" title="Facultative anaerobe">Facultative anaerobes</a> can switch between fermentation and different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terminal_electron_acceptor" title="Terminal electron acceptor">terminal electron acceptors</a> depending on the environmental conditions in which they find themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid30862543_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid30862543-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth_and_reproduction">Growth and reproduction</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_growth" title="Bacterial growth">Bacterial growth</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Three_cell_growth_types.svg"><img alt="drawing of showing the processes of binary fission, mitosis, and meiosis" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="694" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Three_cell_growth_types.svg/220px-Three_cell_growth_types.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Three_cell_growth_types.svg/330px-Three_cell_growth_types.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Three_cell_growth_types.svg/440px-Three_cell_growth_types.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Three_cell_growth_types.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Many bacteria reproduce through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_fission" title="Binary fission">binary fission</a>, which is compared to <a href="/wiki/Mitosis" title="Mitosis">mitosis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Meiosis" title="Meiosis">meiosis</a> in this image.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Salmonella_growing_on_XLD_agar.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1345" data-file-width="1483" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Salmonella_growing_on_XLD_agar.JPG/220px-Salmonella_growing_on_XLD_agar.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Salmonella_growing_on_XLD_agar.JPG/330px-Salmonella_growing_on_XLD_agar.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Salmonella_growing_on_XLD_agar.JPG/440px-Salmonella_growing_on_XLD_agar.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Salmonella_growing_on_XLD_agar.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A culture of <i><a href="/wiki/Salmonella" title="Salmonella">Salmonella</a></i></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:101px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:E.-coli-growth.gif"><img alt="E. coli colony" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="109" data-file-width="99" decoding="async" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/27/E.-coli-growth.gif" width="99"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:E.-coli-growth.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A colony of <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">Escherichia coli</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Unlike in multicellular organisms, increases in cell size (<a href="/wiki/Cell_growth" title="Cell growth">cell growth</a>) and reproduction by <a href="/wiki/Cell_division" title="Cell division">cell division</a> are tightly linked in unicellular organisms. Bacteria grow to a fixed size and then reproduce through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_fission" title="Binary fission">binary fission</a>, a form of <a href="/wiki/Asexual_reproduction" title="Asexual reproduction">asexual reproduction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Under optimal conditions, bacteria can grow and divide extremely rapidly, and some bacterial populations can double as quickly as every 17 minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPommerville2014138_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPommerville2014138-112">[112]</a></sup> In cell division, two identical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clone_(genetics)" title="Clone (genetics)">clone</a> daughter cells are produced. Some bacteria, while still reproducing asexually, form more complex reproductive structures that help disperse the newly formed daughter cells. Examples include fruiting body formation by <a href="/wiki/Myxobacteria" title="Myxobacteria">myxobacteria</a> and aerial <a href="/wiki/Hypha" title="Hypha">hyphae</a> formation by <i><a href="/wiki/Streptomyces" title="Streptomyces">Streptomyces</a></i> species, or budding. Budding involves a cell forming a protrusion that breaks away and produces a daughter cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPommerville2014557_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPommerville2014557-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the laboratory, bacteria are usually grown using solid or liquid media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWheelis200842_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWheelis200842-114">[114]</a></sup> Solid <a href="/wiki/Growth_medium" title="Growth medium">growth media</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Agar_plate" title="Agar plate">agar plates</a>, are used to <a href="/wiki/Isolation_(microbiology)" title="Isolation (microbiology)">isolate</a> pure cultures of a bacterial strain. However, liquid growth media are used when the measurement of growth or large volumes of cells are required. Growth in stirred liquid media occurs as an even cell suspension, making the cultures easy to divide and transfer, although isolating single bacteria from liquid media is difficult. The use of selective media (media with specific nutrients added or deficient, or with antibiotics added) can help identify specific organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomson_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomson-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most laboratory techniques for growing bacteria use high levels of nutrients to produce large amounts of cells cheaply and quickly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWheelis200842_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWheelis200842-114">[114]</a></sup> However, in natural environments, nutrients are limited, meaning that bacteria cannot continue to reproduce indefinitely. This nutrient limitation has led the evolution of different growth strategies (see <a href="/wiki/R/K_selection_theory" title="R/K selection theory">r/K selection theory</a>). Some organisms can grow extremely rapidly when nutrients become available, such as the formation of <a href="/wiki/Algal_bloom" title="Algal bloom">algal</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyanobacterial" title="Cyanobacterial">cyanobacterial</a> blooms that often occur in lakes during the summer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Other organisms have adaptations to harsh environments, such as the production of multiple <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a> by streptomyces that inhibit the growth of competing microorganisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> In nature, many organisms live in communities (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Biofilm" title="Biofilm">biofilms</a>) that may allow for increased supply of nutrients and protection from environmental stresses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davey_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Davey-51">[51]</a></sup> These relationships can be essential for growth of a particular organism or group of organisms (<a href="/wiki/Syntrophy" title="Syntrophy">syntrophy</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Bacterial_growth" title="Bacterial growth">Bacterial growth</a> follows four phases. When a population of bacteria first enter a high-nutrient environment that allows growth, the cells need to adapt to their new environment. The first phase of growth is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stationary_phase_(biology)" title="Stationary phase (biology)">lag phase</a>, a period of slow growth when the cells are adapting to the high-nutrient environment and preparing for fast growth. The lag phase has high biosynthesis rates, as proteins necessary for rapid growth are produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The second phase of growth is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stationary_phase_(biology)" title="Stationary phase (biology)">logarithmic phase</a>, also known as the exponential phase. The log phase is marked by rapid <a href="/wiki/Exponential_growth" title="Exponential growth">exponential growth</a>. The rate at which cells grow during this phase is known as the <i>growth rate</i> (<i>k</i>), and the time it takes the cells to double is known as the <i>generation time</i> (<i>g</i>). During log phase, nutrients are metabolised at maximum speed until one of the nutrients is depleted and starts limiting growth. The third phase of growth is the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stationary_phase_(biology)" title="Stationary phase (biology)">stationary phase</a></i> and is caused by depleted nutrients. The cells reduce their metabolic activity and consume non-essential cellular proteins. The stationary phase is a transition from rapid growth to a stress response state and there is increased <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">expression of genes</a> involved in <a href="/wiki/DNA_repair" title="DNA repair">DNA repair</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antioxidant" title="Antioxidant">antioxidant metabolism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Active_transport" title="Active transport">nutrient transport</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> The final phase is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stationary_phase_(biology)" title="Stationary phase (biology)">death phase</a> where the bacteria run out of nutrients and die.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_genetics" title="Bacterial genetics">Bacterial genetics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Escherichia_coli_with_phages.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1196" data-file-width="787" decoding="async" height="334" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Escherichia_coli_with_phages.jpg/220px-Escherichia_coli_with_phages.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Escherichia_coli_with_phages.jpg/330px-Escherichia_coli_with_phages.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Escherichia_coli_with_phages.jpg/440px-Escherichia_coli_with_phages.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Escherichia_coli_with_phages.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Scanning_helium_ion_microscope" title="Scanning helium ion microscope">Helium ion microscopy</a> image showing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T4_phage" title="T4 phage">T4 phage</a> infecting <i>E. coli</i>. Some of the attached phage have contracted tails indicating that they have injected their DNA into the host. The bacterial cells are ~ 0.5 µm wide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Most bacteria have a single circular <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosome</a> that can range in size from only 160,000 <a href="/wiki/Base_pair" title="Base pair">base pairs</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Endosymbiont" title="Endosymbiont">endosymbiotic</a> bacteria <i><a href="/wiki/Candidatus_Carsonella_ruddii" title="Candidatus Carsonella ruddii">Carsonella ruddii</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> to 12,200,000 base pairs (12.2 Mbp) in the soil-dwelling bacteria <i><a href="/wiki/Sorangium_cellulosum" title="Sorangium cellulosum">Sorangium cellulosum</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> There are many exceptions to this, for example some <i><a href="/wiki/Streptomyces" title="Streptomyces">Streptomyces</a></i> and  <i><a href="/wiki/Borrelia" title="Borrelia">Borrelia</a></i> species contain a single linear chromosome,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> while some <i><a href="/wiki/Vibrio" title="Vibrio">Vibrio</a></i> species contain more than one chromosome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-128">[128]</a></sup> Bacteria can also contain <a href="/wiki/Plasmid" title="Plasmid">plasmids</a>, small extra-chromosomal molecules of DNA that may contain genes for various useful functions such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic_resistance" title="Antibiotic resistance">antibiotic resistance</a>, metabolic capabilities, or various <a href="/wiki/Virulence" title="Virulence">virulence factors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26104369_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26104369-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bacteria genomes usually encode a few hundred to a few thousand genes. The genes in bacterial genomes are usually a single continuous stretch of DNA and although several different types of <a href="/wiki/Intron" title="Intron">introns</a> do exist in bacteria, these are much rarer than in eukaryotes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bacteria, as asexual organisms, inherit an identical copy of the parent's genomes and are <a href="/wiki/Clonal_colony" title="Clonal colony">clonal</a>. However, all bacteria can evolve by selection on changes to their genetic material <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> caused by <a href="/wiki/Genetic_recombination" title="Genetic recombination">genetic recombination</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">mutations</a>. Mutations come from errors made during the replication of DNA or from exposure to <a href="/wiki/Mutagen" title="Mutagen">mutagens</a>. Mutation rates vary widely among different species of bacteria and even among different clones of a single species of bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Genetic changes in bacterial genomes come from either random mutation during replication or "stress-directed mutation", where genes involved in a particular growth-limiting process have an increased mutation rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some bacteria also transfer genetic material between cells. This can occur in three main ways. First, bacteria can take up exogenous DNA from their environment, in a process called <a href="/wiki/Transformation_(genetics)" title="Transformation (genetics)">transformation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> Many bacteria can <a href="/wiki/Natural_competence" title="Natural competence">naturally</a> take up DNA from the environment, while others must be chemically altered in order to induce them to take up DNA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> The development of competence in nature is usually associated with stressful environmental conditions, and seems to be an adaptation for facilitating repair of DNA damage in recipient cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> The second way bacteria transfer genetic material is by <a href="/wiki/Transduction_(genetics)" title="Transduction (genetics)">transduction</a>, when the integration of a <a href="/wiki/Bacteriophage" title="Bacteriophage">bacteriophage</a> introduces foreign DNA into the chromosome. Many types of bacteriophage exist, some simply infect and <a href="/wiki/Lytic_cycle" title="Lytic cycle">lyse</a> their <a href="/wiki/Host_(biology)" title="Host (biology)">host</a> bacteria, while others insert into the bacterial chromosome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> Bacteria resist phage infection through <a href="/wiki/Restriction_modification_system" title="Restriction modification system">restriction modification systems</a> that degrade foreign DNA,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> and a system that uses <a href="/wiki/CRISPR" title="CRISPR">CRISPR</a> sequences to retain fragments of the genomes of phage that the bacteria have come into contact with in the past, which allows them to block virus replication through a form of <a href="/wiki/RNA_interference" title="RNA interference">RNA interference</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> The third method of gene transfer is <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_conjugation" title="Bacterial conjugation">conjugation</a>, whereby DNA is transferred through direct cell contact. In ordinary circumstances, transduction, conjugation, and transformation involve transfer of DNA between individual bacteria of the same species, but occasionally transfer may occur between individuals of different bacterial species and this may have significant consequences, such as the transfer of antibiotic resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> In such cases, gene acquisition from other bacteria or the environment is called <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_gene_transfer" title="Horizontal gene transfer">horizontal gene transfer</a> and may be common under natural conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour">Behaviour</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Movement">Movement</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_motility" title="Bacterial motility">Bacterial motility</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dvulgaris_micrograph.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1132" data-file-width="1276" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Dvulgaris_micrograph.JPG/220px-Dvulgaris_micrograph.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Dvulgaris_micrograph.JPG/330px-Dvulgaris_micrograph.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Dvulgaris_micrograph.JPG/440px-Dvulgaris_micrograph.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dvulgaris_micrograph.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Transmission electron micrograph of <i><a href="/wiki/Desulfovibrio_vulgaris" title="Desulfovibrio vulgaris">Desulfovibrio vulgaris</a></i> showing a single flagellum at one end of the cell. Scale bar is 0.5 micrometers long.</div></div></div>
<p>Many bacteria are <a href="/wiki/Motility" title="Motility">motile</a> (able to move themselves) and do so using a variety of mechanisms. The best studied of these are <a href="/wiki/Flagellum" title="Flagellum">flagella</a>, long filaments that are turned by a motor at the base to generate propeller-like movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bardy_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bardy-143">[143]</a></sup> The bacterial flagellum is made of about 20 proteins, with approximately another 30 proteins required for its regulation and assembly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bardy_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bardy-143">[143]</a></sup> The flagellum is a rotating structure driven by a reversible motor at the base that uses the <a href="/wiki/Electrochemical_gradient" title="Electrochemical gradient">electrochemical gradient</a> across the membrane for power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flagella.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="471" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Flagella.png/220px-Flagella.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Flagella.png/330px-Flagella.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/08/Flagella.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flagella.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The different arrangements of bacterial flagella: A-Monotrichous; B-Lophotrichous; C-Amphitrichous; D-Peritrichous</div></div></div>
<p>Bacteria can use flagella in different ways to generate different kinds of movement. Many bacteria (such as <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">E. coli</a></i>) have two distinct modes of movement: forward movement (swimming) and tumbling. The tumbling allows them to reorient and makes their movement a three-dimensional <a href="/wiki/Random_walk" title="Random walk">random walk</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Bacterial species differ in the number and arrangement of flagella on their surface; some have a single flagellum (<i><a href="/wiki/Flagellum#Flagellar_arrangement_schemes" title="Flagellum">monotrichous</a></i>), a flagellum at each end (<i><a href="/wiki/Flagellum#Flagellar_arrangement_schemes" title="Flagellum">amphitrichous</a></i>), clusters of flagella at the poles of the cell (<i><a href="/wiki/Flagellum#Flagellar_arrangement_schemes" title="Flagellum">lophotrichous</a></i>), while others have flagella distributed over the entire surface of the cell (<i><a href="/wiki/Flagellum#Flagellar_arrangement_schemes" title="Flagellum">peritrichous</a></i>). The flagella of a unique group of bacteria, the <a href="/wiki/Spirochaete" title="Spirochaete">spirochaetes</a>, are found between two membranes in the periplasmic space. They have a distinctive <a href="/wiki/Helix" title="Helix">helical</a> body that twists about as it moves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bardy_143-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bardy-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>Two other types of bacterial motion are called <a href="/wiki/Twitching_motility" title="Twitching motility">twitching motility</a> that relies on a structure called the <a href="/wiki/Pilus#Type_IV_pili" title="Pilus">type IV pilus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacterial_gliding" title="Bacterial gliding">gliding motility</a>, that uses other mechanisms. In twitching motility, the rod-like pilus extends out from the cell, binds some substrate, and then retracts, pulling the cell forward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>Motile bacteria are attracted or repelled by certain <a href="/wiki/Stimulus_(physiology)" title="Stimulus (physiology)">stimuli</a> in behaviours called <i><a href="/wiki/Taxis" title="Taxis">taxes</a></i>: these include <a href="/wiki/Chemotaxis" title="Chemotaxis">chemotaxis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phototaxis" title="Phototaxis">phototaxis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taxis" title="Taxis">energy taxis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Magnetotaxis" title="Magnetotaxis">magnetotaxis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> In one peculiar group, the myxobacteria, individual bacteria move together to form waves of cells that then differentiate to form fruiting bodies containing spores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaiser_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kaiser-46">[46]</a></sup> The myxobacteria move only when on solid surfaces, unlike <i>E. coli</i>, which is motile in liquid or solid media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21910630_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21910630-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several <i><a href="/wiki/Listeria" title="Listeria">Listeria</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Shigella" title="Shigella">Shigella</a></i> species move inside host cells by usurping the <a href="/wiki/Cytoskeleton" title="Cytoskeleton">cytoskeleton</a>, which is normally used to move <a href="/wiki/Organelle" title="Organelle">organelles</a> inside the cell. By promoting <a href="/wiki/Actin" title="Actin">actin</a> <a href="/wiki/Biopolymer" title="Biopolymer">polymerisation</a> at one pole of their cells, they can form a kind of tail that pushes them through the host cell's cytoplasm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Communication">Communication</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote#Sociality" title="Prokaryote">Prokaryote § Sociality</a></div>
<p>A few bacteria have chemical systems that generate light. This <a href="/wiki/Bioluminescence" title="Bioluminescence">bioluminescence</a> often occurs in bacteria that live in association with fish, and the light probably serves to attract fish or other large animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31817999_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31817999-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bacteria often function as multicellular aggregates known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biofilms" title="Biofilms">biofilms</a>, exchanging a variety of molecular signals for <a href="/wiki/Cell_signaling" title="Cell signaling">inter-cell communication</a>, and engaging in coordinated multicellular behaviour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro1_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro1-154">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-costerton1_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-costerton1-155">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>The communal benefits of multicellular cooperation include a cellular division of labour, accessing resources that cannot effectively be used by single cells, collectively defending against antagonists, and optimising population survival by differentiating into distinct cell types.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro1_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro1-154">[154]</a></sup> For example, bacteria in biofilms can have more than 500 times increased resistance to <a href="/wiki/Bactericide" title="Bactericide">antibacterial</a> agents than individual "planktonic" bacteria of the same species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-costerton1_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-costerton1-155">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>One type of inter-cellular communication by a molecular signal is called <a href="/wiki/Quorum_sensing" title="Quorum sensing">quorum sensing</a>, which serves the purpose of determining whether there is a local population density that is sufficiently high that it is productive to invest in processes that are only successful if large numbers of similar organisms behave similarly, as in excreting digestive enzymes or emitting light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31732932_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31732932-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid29789364_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid29789364-157">[157]</a></sup>
</p><p>Quorum sensing allows bacteria to coordinate <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">gene expression</a>, and enables them to produce, release and detect <a href="/wiki/Autoinducer" title="Autoinducer">autoinducers</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pheromones" title="Pheromones">pheromones</a> which accumulate with the growth in cell population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11544353_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11544353-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification_and_identification">Classification and identification</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_taxonomy" title="Bacterial taxonomy">Bacterial taxonomy</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_classification" title="Scientific classification">Scientific classification</a>, <a href="/wiki/Systematics" title="Systematics">Systematics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_phyla" title="Bacterial phyla">Bacterial phyla</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Clinical_pathology" title="Clinical pathology">Clinical pathology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Streptococcus_mutans_Gram.jpg"><img alt="blue stain of Streptococcus mutans" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Streptococcus_mutans_Gram.jpg/220px-Streptococcus_mutans_Gram.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Streptococcus_mutans_Gram.jpg/330px-Streptococcus_mutans_Gram.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Streptococcus_mutans_Gram.jpg/440px-Streptococcus_mutans_Gram.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Streptococcus_mutans_Gram.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Streptococcus mutans</i> visualised with a Gram stain.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anillo_de_la_vida.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="820" data-file-width="1107" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Anillo_de_la_vida.png/300px-Anillo_de_la_vida.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Anillo_de_la_vida.png/450px-Anillo_de_la_vida.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Anillo_de_la_vida.png/600px-Anillo_de_la_vida.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anillo_de_la_vida.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree" title="Phylogenetic tree">Phylogenetic tree</a> showing the diversity of bacteria, compared to other organisms. Here bacteria are represented by three main supergroups: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CPR_group" title="CPR group">CPR</a> <a href="/wiki/Ultramicrobacteria" title="Ultramicrobacteria">ultramicrobacterias</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terrabacteria" title="Terrabacteria">Terrabacteria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gracilicutes" title="Gracilicutes">Gracilicutes</a> according to recent genomic analyzes (2019).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhu_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhu-159">[159]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_classification" title="Scientific classification">Classification</a> seeks to describe the diversity of bacterial species by naming and grouping organisms based on similarities. Bacteria can be classified on the basis of cell structure, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cell_metabolism" title="Cell metabolism">cellular metabolism</a> or on differences in cell components, such as <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a>, pigments, <a href="/wiki/Antigen" title="Antigen">antigens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quinone" title="Quinone">quinones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomson_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thomson-115">[115]</a></sup> While these schemes allowed the identification and classification of bacterial strains, it was unclear whether these differences represented variation between distinct species or between strains of the same species. This uncertainty was due to the lack of distinctive structures in most bacteria, as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lateral_gene_transfer" title="Lateral gene transfer">lateral gene transfer</a> between unrelated species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> Due to lateral gene transfer, some closely related bacteria can have very different morphologies and metabolisms. To overcome this uncertainty, modern bacterial classification emphasises <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_systematics" title="Molecular systematics">molecular systematics</a>, using genetic techniques such as <a href="/wiki/Guanine" title="Guanine">guanine</a> <a href="/wiki/Cytosine" title="Cytosine">cytosine</a> <a href="/wiki/GC-content" title="GC-content">ratio</a> determination, genome-genome hybridisation, as well as <a href="/wiki/DNA_sequencing" title="DNA sequencing">sequencing</a> genes that have not undergone extensive lateral gene transfer, such as the <a href="/wiki/Ribosomal_DNA" title="Ribosomal DNA">rRNA gene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> Classification of bacteria is determined by publication in the International Journal of Systematic Bacteriology,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> and Bergey's Manual of Systematic Bacteriology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Committee_on_Systematic_Bacteriology" title="International Committee on Systematic Bacteriology">International Committee on Systematic Bacteriology</a> (ICSB) maintains international rules for the naming of bacteria and taxonomic categories and for the ranking of them in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Code_of_Nomenclature_of_Bacteria" title="International Code of Nomenclature of Bacteria">International Code of Nomenclature of Bacteria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid25921438_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid25921438-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historically, bacteria were considered a part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plants" title="Plants">Plantae</a>, the Plant kingdom, and were called "Schizomycetes" (fission-fungi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> For this reason, collective bacteria and other microorganisms in a host are often called "flora".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid33180890_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid33180890-166">[166]</a></sup>
The term "bacteria" was traditionally applied to all microscopic, single-cell prokaryotes. However, molecular systematics showed prokaryotic life to consist of two separate <a href="/wiki/Domain_(biology)" title="Domain (biology)">domains</a>, originally called Eubacteria and Archaebacteria, but now called Bacteria and Archaea that evolved independently from an ancient common ancestor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHall2008145_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHall2008145-3">[3]</a></sup> The archaea and eukaryotes are more closely related to each other than either is to the bacteria. These two domains, along with Eukarya, are the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Three-domain_system" title="Three-domain system">three-domain system</a>, which is currently the most widely used classification system in microbiology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta-167">[167]</a></sup> However, due to the relatively recent introduction of molecular systematics and a rapid increase in the number of genome sequences that are available, bacterial classification remains a changing and expanding field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rappe_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rappe-168">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cavalier-Smith" title="Thomas Cavalier-Smith">Cavalier-Smith</a> argued that the Archaea and Eukaryotes evolved from Gram-positive bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cavalier-Smith2002_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cavalier-Smith2002-170">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>The identification of bacteria in the laboratory is particularly relevant in <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>, where the correct treatment is determined by the bacterial species causing an infection. Consequently, the need to identify human pathogens was a major impetus for the development of techniques to identify bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPommerville201415−31_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPommerville201415−31-171">[171]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Gram_stain" title="Gram stain">Gram stain</a></i>, developed in 1884 by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Christian_Gram" title="Hans Christian Gram">Hans Christian Gram</a>, characterises bacteria based on the structural characteristics of their cell walls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrasner201477_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKrasner201477-172">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gram_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gram-73">[73]</a></sup> The thick layers of peptidoglycan in the "Gram-positive" cell wall stain purple, while the thin "Gram-negative" cell wall appears pink.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrasner201477_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKrasner201477-172">[172]</a></sup> By combining morphology and Gram-staining, most bacteria can be classified as belonging to one of four groups (Gram-positive cocci, Gram-positive bacilli, Gram-negative cocci and Gram-negative bacilli). Some organisms are best identified by stains other than the Gram stain, particularly mycobacteria or <i>Nocardia</i>, which show <a href="/wiki/Acid-fastness" title="Acid-fastness">acid fastness</a> on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ziehl-Neelsen_stain" title="Ziehl-Neelsen stain">Ziehl–Neelsen</a> or similar stains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> Other organisms may need to be identified by their growth in special media, or by other techniques, such as <a href="/wiki/Serology" title="Serology">serology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31354679_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31354679-174">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Microbiological_culture" title="Microbiological culture">Culture</a> techniques are designed to promote the growth and identify particular bacteria, while restricting the growth of the other bacteria in the sample.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrasner201487–89_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKrasner201487–89-175">[175]</a></sup> Often these techniques are designed for specific specimens; for example, a <a href="/wiki/Sputum" title="Sputum">sputum</a> sample will be treated to identify organisms that cause <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Feces" title="Feces">stool</a> specimens are cultured on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selective_media" title="Selective media">selective media</a> to identify organisms that cause <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a>, while preventing growth of non-pathogenic bacteria. Specimens that are normally sterile, such as <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urine" title="Urine">urine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cerebrospinal_fluid" title="Cerebrospinal fluid">spinal fluid</a>, are cultured under conditions designed to grow all possible organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomson_115-2"><a href="#cite_note-Thomson-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> Once a pathogenic organism has been isolated, it can be further characterised by its morphology, growth patterns (such as <a href="/wiki/Aerobic_organism" title="Aerobic organism">aerobic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_organism" title="Anaerobic organism">anaerobic</a> growth), <a href="/wiki/Hemolysis_(microbiology)" title="Hemolysis (microbiology)">patterns of hemolysis</a>, and staining.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid30387415_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid30387415-177">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p>As with bacterial classification, identification of bacteria is increasingly using molecular methods,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid33809163_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid33809163-178">[178]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Matrix-assisted_laser_desorption/ionization" title="Matrix-assisted laser desorption/ionization">mass spectroscopy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid34041492_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid34041492-179">[179]</a></sup> Most bacteria have not been characterised and there are may species that cannot be <a href="/wiki/Microbiological_culture" title="Microbiological culture">grown</a> in the laboratory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dudek_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dudek-180">[180]</a></sup> Diagnostics using DNA-based tools, such as <a href="/wiki/Polymerase_chain_reaction" title="Polymerase chain reaction">polymerase chain reaction</a>, are increasingly popular due to their specificity and speed, compared to culture-based methods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> These methods also allow the detection and identification of "<a href="/wiki/Viable_but_nonculturable" title="Viable but nonculturable">viable but nonculturable</a>" cells that are metabolically active but non-dividing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> However, even using these improved methods, the total number of bacterial species is not known and cannot even be estimated with any certainty. Following present classification, there are a little less than 9,300 known species of prokaryotes, which includes bacteria and archaea;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> but attempts to estimate the true number of bacterial diversity have ranged from 10<sup>7</sup> to 10<sup>9</sup> total species—and even these diverse estimates may be off by many orders of magnitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup>
</p>
