<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genera_incertae_sedis">Genera <i>incertae sedis</i></span></h3>
<p>The following bacteria genera have not been assigned to a phylum, class, or order:
</p>
<ul><li>"<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Fermentobadaceae&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Fermentobadaceae (page does not exist)">Fermentobadaceae</a>" <small>Haiying 1995</small>
<ul><li>"<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Guhaiyingella&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Guhaiyingella (page does not exist)">Guhaiyingella</a>" <small>Haiying 1995</small></li></ul></li>
<li>Not assigned to a family:
<ul><li>"<i>Candidatus</i> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Aegiribacteria&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Aegiribacteria (page does not exist)">Aegiribacteria</a>" <small>Hamilton et al. 2016</small></li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Archaeoscillatoriopsis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Archaeoscillatoriopsis (page does not exist)">Archaeoscillatoriopsis</a> <small>Schopf 1993</small></li>
<li>"<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Eoleptonema&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Eoleptonema (page does not exist)">Eoleptonema</a>" <small>Awramik et al. 1983</small></li>
<li>"<i>Candidatus</i> <a href="/wiki/Epulonipiscium" title="Epulonipiscium">Epulonipiscium</a>" <small>corrig. Montgomery and Pollak 1988</small></li>
<li>"<i>Candidatus</i> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ovibacter&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ovibacter (page does not exist)">Ovibacter</a>" <small>corrig. Fenchel and Thar 2004</small></li>
<li>"<i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Primaevifilum&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Primaevifilum (page does not exist)">Primaevifilum</a></i>" <small>Schopf 1983</small></li>
<li>"<i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rappaport_(bacterium)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rappaport (bacterium) (page does not exist)">Rappaport</a></i>" <small>Waldman Ben-Asher et al. 2017</small></li></ul></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interactions_with_other_organisms">Interactions with other organisms</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microbes_in_human_culture" title="Microbes in human culture">Microbes in human culture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bacterial_infections_and_involved_species.png"><img alt="chart showing bacterial infections upon various parts of human body" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2709" data-file-width="2746" decoding="async" height="296" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Bacterial_infections_and_involved_species.png/300px-Bacterial_infections_and_involved_species.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Bacterial_infections_and_involved_species.png/450px-Bacterial_infections_and_involved_species.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Bacterial_infections_and_involved_species.png/600px-Bacterial_infections_and_involved_species.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bacterial_infections_and_involved_species.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Overview of bacterial infections and main species involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Microbiology33_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-Microbiology33-191">[191]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Despite their apparent simplicity, bacteria can form complex associations with other organisms. These <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">symbiotic</a> associations can be divided into <a href="/wiki/Parasitism" title="Parasitism">parasitism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)" title="Mutualism (biology)">mutualism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commensalism" title="Commensalism">commensalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26568407_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26568407-192">[192]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commensals">Commensals</span></h3>
<p>The word "<a href="/wiki/Commensalism" title="Commensalism">commensalism</a>" is derived from the word "commensal", meaning "eating at the same table"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> and all plants and animals are colonised by commensal bacteria. In humans and other animals millions of them live on the skin, the airways, the gut and other orifices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31214175_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31214175-195">[195]</a></sup>
Referred to as "normal flora",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid3074102_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid3074102-196">[196]</a></sup> or "commensals",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid34329585_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid34329585-197">[197]</a></sup> these bacteria usually cause no harm but may occasionally invade other sites of the body and cause infection.  <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">Escherichia coli</a></i> is a commensal in the human gut but can cause urinary tract infections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid33575225_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid33575225-198">[198]</a></sup> Similarly, streptoccoci, which are part of the normal flora of the human mouth, can cause <a href="/wiki/Subacute_bacterial_endocarditis" title="Subacute bacterial endocarditis">heart disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid33852085_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid33852085-199">[199]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Predators">Predators</span></h3>
<p>Some species of bacteria kill and then consume other microorganisms, these species are called <i>predatory bacteria</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> These include organisms such as <i><a href="/wiki/Myxococcus_xanthus" title="Myxococcus xanthus">Myxococcus xanthus</a></i>, which forms swarms of cells that kill and digest any bacteria they encounter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> Other bacterial predators either attach to their prey in order to digest them and absorb nutrients or invade another cell and multiply inside the cytosol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid34010833_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid34010833-202">[202]</a></sup> These predatory bacteria are thought to have evolved from <a href="/wiki/Detritivore" title="Detritivore">saprophages</a> that consumed dead microorganisms, through adaptations that allowed them to entrap and kill other organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19174136_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19174136-203">[203]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mutualists">Mutualists</span></h3>
<p>Certain bacteria form close spatial associations that are essential for their survival. One such mutualistic association, called interspecies hydrogen transfer, occurs between clusters of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaerobic_bacteria" title="Anaerobic bacteria">anaerobic bacteria</a> that consume <a href="/wiki/Organic_acid" title="Organic acid">organic acids</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Butyric_acid" title="Butyric acid">butyric acid</a> or <a href="/wiki/Propionic_acid" title="Propionic acid">propionic acid</a>, and produce <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">methanogenic</a> archaea that consume hydrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> The bacteria in this association are unable to consume the organic acids as this reaction produces hydrogen that accumulates in their surroundings. Only the intimate association with the hydrogen-consuming archaea keeps the hydrogen concentration low enough to allow the bacteria to grow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid29411546_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid29411546-205">[205]</a></sup>
</p><p>In soil, microorganisms that reside in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhizosphere_(ecology)" title="Rhizosphere (ecology)">rhizosphere</a> (a zone that includes the <a href="/wiki/Root" title="Root">root</a> surface and the soil that adheres to the root after gentle shaking) carry out <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation" title="Nitrogen fixation">nitrogen fixation</a>, converting nitrogen gas to nitrogenous compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> This serves to provide an easily absorbable form of nitrogen for many plants, which cannot fix nitrogen themselves. Many other bacteria are found as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbiont" title="Symbiont">symbionts</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacteria_in_the_human_body" title="Bacteria in the human body">in humans</a> and other organisms. For example, the presence of over 1,000 bacterial species in the normal human <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gut_flora" title="Gut flora">gut flora</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intestine" title="Intestine">intestines</a> can contribute to gut immunity, synthesise <a href="/wiki/Vitamin" title="Vitamin">vitamins</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Folic_acid" title="Folic acid">folic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_K" title="Vitamin K">vitamin K</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biotin" title="Biotin">biotin</a>, convert <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milk_protein" title="Milk protein">sugars</a> to <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactic acid</a> (see <i><a href="/wiki/Lactobacillus" title="Lactobacillus">Lactobacillus</a></i>), as well as fermenting complex undigestible <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> The presence of this gut flora also inhibits the growth of potentially pathogenic bacteria (usually through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Competitive_exclusion" title="Competitive exclusion">competitive exclusion</a>) and these beneficial bacteria are consequently sold as <a href="/wiki/Probiotic" title="Probiotic">probiotic</a> <a href="/wiki/Dietary_supplement" title="Dietary supplement">dietary supplements</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nearly all <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">animal life</a> is dependent on bacteria for survival as only bacteria and some <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a> possess the genes and enzymes necessary to synthesize <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_B12" title="Vitamin B12">vitamin B<sub>12</sub></a>, also known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cobalamin" title="Cobalamin">cobalamin</a>, and provide it through the food chain.  Vitamin B<sub>12</sub> is a water-soluble <a href="/wiki/Vitamin" title="Vitamin">vitamin</a> that is involved in the <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a> of every cell of the human body.  It is a <a href="/wiki/Cofactor_(biochemistry)" title="Cofactor (biochemistry)">cofactor</a> in <a href="/wiki/DNA_replication" title="DNA replication">DNA synthesis</a>, and in both <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid_metabolism" title="Fatty acid metabolism">fatty acid</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amino_acid_metabolism" title="Amino acid metabolism">amino acid metabolism</a>. It is particularly important in the normal functioning of the <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">nervous system</a> via its role in the <a href="/wiki/Myelinogenesis" title="Myelinogenesis">synthesis of myelin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid29216732_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid29216732-211">[211]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pathogens">Pathogens</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pathogenic_bacteria" title="Pathogenic bacteria">Pathogenic bacteria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SalmonellaNIAID.jpg"><img alt="Color-enhanced scanning electron micrograph of red Salmonella typhimurium in yellow human cells" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1761" data-file-width="2100" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/SalmonellaNIAID.jpg/220px-SalmonellaNIAID.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/SalmonellaNIAID.jpg/330px-SalmonellaNIAID.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/SalmonellaNIAID.jpg/440px-SalmonellaNIAID.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SalmonellaNIAID.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Colour-enhanced scanning electron micrograph showing <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salmonella_typhimurium" title="Salmonella typhimurium">Salmonella typhimurium</a></i> (red) invading cultured human cells</div></div></div>
<p>The body is continually exposed to many species of bacteria, including beneficial commensals, which grow on the skin and <a href="/wiki/Mucous_membrane" title="Mucous membrane">mucous membranes</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saprophyte" title="Saprophyte">saprophytes</a>, which grow mainly in the soil and in <a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">decaying</a> matter. The blood and tissue fluids contain nutrients sufficient to sustain the growth of many bacteria. The body has  defence mechanisms that enable it  to resist microbial invasion of its tissues and give it a natural <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immunity</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Innate_immunity" title="Innate immunity">innate resistance</a> against many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microorganisms" title="Microorganisms">microorganisms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPommerville201416–21_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPommerville201416–21-212">[212]</a></sup> Unlike some <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a>, bacteria evolve relatively slowly so many bacterial diseases also occur in other animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClark2010215_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClark2010215-213">[213]</a></sup>
</p><p>If bacteria form a parasitic association with other organisms, they are classed as pathogens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWheelis200844_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWheelis200844-214">[214]</a></sup> Pathogenic bacteria are a major cause of human death and disease and cause infections such as <a href="/wiki/Tetanus" title="Tetanus">tetanus</a> (caused by <i><a href="/wiki/Clostridium_tetani" title="Clostridium tetani">Clostridium tetani</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Typhoid_fever" title="Typhoid fever">typhoid fever</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diphtheria" title="Diphtheria">diphtheria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">syphilis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Foodborne_illness" title="Foodborne illness">foodborne illness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leprosy" title="Leprosy">leprosy</a> (caused by <i><a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_leprae" title="Mycobacterium leprae">Mycobacterium leprae</a></i>) and <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a> (caused by <i><a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_tuberculosis" title="Mycobacterium tuberculosis">Mycobacterium tuberculosis</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClark201030,_195,_233,_236_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClark201030,_195,_233,_236-215">[215]</a></sup>  A pathogenic cause for a known medical disease may only be discovered many years later, as was the case with <i><a href="/wiki/Helicobacter_pylori" title="Helicobacter pylori">Helicobacter pylori</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_peptic_ulcer_disease_and_Helicobacter_pylori" title="Timeline of peptic ulcer disease and Helicobacter pylori">peptic ulcer disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid34244666_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid34244666-216">[216]</a></sup> Bacterial diseases are also important in <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>, with bacteria causing <a href="/wiki/Leaf_spot" title="Leaf spot">leaf spot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fire_blight" title="Fire blight">fire blight</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wilting" title="Wilting">wilts</a> in plants, as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johne%27s_disease" title="Johne's disease">Johne's disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mastitis_in_dairy_cattle" title="Mastitis in dairy cattle">mastitis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salmonellosis" title="Salmonellosis">salmonella</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anthrax" title="Anthrax">anthrax</a> in farm animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid27660260_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid27660260-217">[217]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Normal_and_BV_flora.jpg"><img alt="Gram-stained micrograph of bacteria from the vagina" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="762" data-file-width="568" decoding="async" height="295" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Normal_and_BV_flora.jpg/220px-Normal_and_BV_flora.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Normal_and_BV_flora.jpg/330px-Normal_and_BV_flora.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Normal_and_BV_flora.jpg/440px-Normal_and_BV_flora.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Normal_and_BV_flora.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_vaginosis" title="Bacterial vaginosis">bacterial vaginosis</a>, beneficial bacteria in the vagina (top) are displaced by pathogens (bottom). Gram stain.</div></div></div>
<p>Each species of pathogen has a characteristic spectrum of interactions with its human <a href="/wiki/Host_(biology)" title="Host (biology)">hosts</a>. Some organisms, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Staphylococcus" title="Staphylococcus">Staphylococcus</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Streptococcus" title="Streptococcus">Streptococcus</a></i>, can cause skin infections, <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meningitis" title="Meningitis">meningitis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sepsis" title="Sepsis">sepsis</a>, a systemic <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammatory response</a> producing <a href="/wiki/Shock_(circulatory)" title="Shock (circulatory)">shock</a>, massive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vasodilator" title="Vasodilator">vasodilation</a> and death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> Yet these organisms are also part of the normal human flora and usually exist on the skin or in the nose without causing any disease at all. Other organisms invariably cause disease in humans, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Rickettsia" title="Rickettsia">Rickettsia</a></i>, which are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Obligate_intracellular_parasite" title="Obligate intracellular parasite">obligate intracellular parasites</a> able to grow and reproduce only within the cells of other organisms. One species of <i>Rickettsia</i> causes <a href="/wiki/Typhus" title="Typhus">typhus</a>, while another causes <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountain_spotted_fever" title="Rocky Mountain spotted fever">Rocky Mountain spotted fever</a>. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chlamydia_(bacterium)" title="Chlamydia (bacterium)">Chlamydia</a></i>, another phylum of obligate intracellular parasites, contains species that can cause pneumonia or <a href="/wiki/Urinary_tract_infection" title="Urinary tract infection">urinary tract infection</a> and may be involved in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coronary_heart_disease" title="Coronary heart disease">coronary heart disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> Some species, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudomonas_aeruginosa" title="Pseudomonas aeruginosa">Pseudomonas aeruginosa</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Burkholderia_cenocepacia" title="Burkholderia cenocepacia">Burkholderia cenocepacia</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_avium_complex" title="Mycobacterium avium complex">Mycobacterium avium</a></i>, are <a href="/wiki/Opportunistic_infection" title="Opportunistic infection">opportunistic pathogens</a> and cause disease mainly in people who are <a href="/wiki/Immunosuppression" title="Immunosuppression">immunosuppressed</a> or have <a href="/wiki/Cystic_fibrosis" title="Cystic fibrosis">cystic fibrosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saiman_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saiman-221">[221]</a></sup> Some bacteria  produce <a href="/wiki/Microbial_toxin" title="Microbial toxin">toxins</a>, which cause diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPommerville2014118_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPommerville2014118-222">[222]</a></sup> These are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endotoxin" title="Endotoxin">endotoxins</a>, which come from broken bacterial cells, and <a href="/wiki/Exotoxin" title="Exotoxin">exotoxins</a>,  which are produced by bacteria and released into the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPommerville2014646–47_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPommerville2014646–47-223">[223]</a></sup> The bacterium <i><a href="/wiki/Clostridium_botulinum" title="Clostridium botulinum">Clostridium botulinum</a></i> for example, produces a powerful exotoxin that cause respiratory paralysis, and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salmonellae" title="Salmonellae">Salmonellae</a></i> produce an endotoxin that causes gastroenteritis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPommerville2014646–47_223-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPommerville2014646–47-223">[223]</a></sup> Some exotoxins can be converted to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toxoids" title="Toxoids">toxoids</a>, which are used as vaccines to prevent the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrasner2014165,_369_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKrasner2014165,_369-224">[224]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bacterial infections may be treated with <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a>, which are classified as <a href="/wiki/Bactericide" title="Bactericide">bacteriocidal</a> if they kill bacteria or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacteriostatic" title="Bacteriostatic">bacteriostatic</a> if they just prevent bacterial growth. There are many types of antibiotics, and each class <a href="/wiki/Enzyme_inhibitor" title="Enzyme inhibitor">inhibits</a> a process that is different in the pathogen from that found in the host. An example of how antibiotics produce selective toxicity are <a href="/wiki/Chloramphenicol" title="Chloramphenicol">chloramphenicol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Puromycin" title="Puromycin">puromycin</a>, which inhibit the bacterial <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosome</a>, but not the structurally different eukaryotic ribosome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> Antibiotics are used both in treating human disease and in <a href="/wiki/Intensive_farming" title="Intensive farming">intensive farming</a> to promote animal growth, where they may be contributing to the rapid development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic_resistance" title="Antibiotic resistance">antibiotic resistance</a> in bacterial populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> Infections can be prevented by <a href="/wiki/Antiseptic" title="Antiseptic">antiseptic</a> measures such as sterilising the skin prior to piercing it with the needle of a syringe, and by proper care of indwelling catheters. Surgical and dental instruments are also <a href="/wiki/Sterilization_(microbiology)" title="Sterilization (microbiology)">sterilised</a> to prevent contamination by bacteria. <a href="/wiki/Disinfectant" title="Disinfectant">Disinfectants</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Bleach" title="Bleach">bleach</a> are used to kill bacteria or other pathogens on surfaces to prevent contamination and further reduce the risk of infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid28954657_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid28954657-227">[227]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Significance_in_technology_and_industry">Significance in technology and industry</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Economic_importance_of_bacteria" title="Economic importance of bacteria">Economic importance of bacteria</a></div>
<p>Bacteria, often <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid_bacteria" title="Lactic acid bacteria">lactic acid bacteria</a>, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Lactobacillus" title="Lactobacillus">Lactobacillus</a></i> species and <i><a href="/wiki/Lactococcus" title="Lactococcus">Lactococcus</a></i> species, in combination with <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeasts</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mold_(fungus)" title="Mold (fungus)">moulds</a>, have been used for thousands of years in the preparation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(food)" title="Fermentation (food)">fermented</a> foods, such as <a href="/wiki/Cheese" title="Cheese">cheese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pickling" title="Pickling">pickles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">soy sauce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sauerkraut" title="Sauerkraut">sauerkraut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vinegar" title="Vinegar">vinegar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">wine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yogurt" title="Yogurt">yogurt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrasner201425–26_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKrasner201425–26-229">[229]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ability of bacteria to degrade a variety of organic compounds is remarkable and has been used in waste processing and <a href="/wiki/Bioremediation" title="Bioremediation">bioremediation</a>. Bacteria capable of digesting the <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> in <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> are often used to clean up <a href="/wiki/Oil_spill" title="Oil spill">oil spills</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> Fertiliser was added to some of the beaches in <a href="/wiki/Prince_William_Sound" title="Prince William Sound">Prince William Sound</a> in an attempt to promote the growth of these naturally occurring bacteria after the 1989 <a href="/wiki/Exxon_Valdez_oil_spill" title="Exxon Valdez oil spill"><i>Exxon Valdez</i> oil spill</a>. These efforts were effective on beaches that were not too thickly covered in oil. Bacteria are also used for the <a href="/wiki/Bioremediation" title="Bioremediation">bioremediation</a> of industrial <a href="/wiki/Toxic_waste" title="Toxic waste">toxic wastes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_industry" title="Chemical industry">chemical industry</a>, bacteria are most important in the production of <a href="/wiki/Enantiomer" title="Enantiomer">enantiomerically</a> pure chemicals for use as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical_company" title="Pharmaceutical company">pharmaceuticals</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agrichemical" title="Agrichemical">agrichemicals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bacteria can also be used in the place of <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Biological_pest_control" title="Biological pest control">biological pest control</a>. This commonly involves <i><a href="/wiki/Bacillus_thuringiensis" title="Bacillus thuringiensis">Bacillus thuringiensis</a></i> (also called BT), a Gram-positive, soil dwelling bacterium. Subspecies of this bacteria are used as a <a href="/wiki/Lepidoptera" title="Lepidoptera">Lepidopteran</a>-specific <a href="/wiki/Insecticide" title="Insecticide">insecticides</a> under trade names such as Dipel and Thuricide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup> Because of their specificity, these pesticides are regarded as <a href="/wiki/Environmentally_friendly" title="Environmentally friendly">environmentally friendly</a>, with little or no effect on humans, <a href="/wiki/Wildlife" title="Wildlife">wildlife</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pollinator" title="Pollinator">pollinators</a> and most other <a href="/wiki/Beneficial_insect" title="Beneficial insect">beneficial insects</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of their ability to quickly grow and the relative ease with which they can be manipulated, bacteria are the workhorses for the fields of <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemistry</a>. By making mutations in bacterial DNA and examining the resulting phenotypes, scientists can determine the function of genes, <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_pathway" title="Metabolic pathway">metabolic pathways</a> in bacteria, then apply this knowledge to more complex organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup> This aim of understanding the biochemistry of a cell reaches its most complex expression in the synthesis of huge amounts of <a href="/wiki/Enzyme_kinetics" title="Enzyme kinetics">enzyme kinetic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">gene expression</a> data into <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_model" title="Mathematical model">mathematical models</a> of entire organisms. This is achievable in some well-studied bacteria, with models of <i>Escherichia coli</i> metabolism now being produced and tested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid14985762_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid14985762-237">[237]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup> This understanding of bacterial metabolism and genetics allows the use of biotechnology to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bioengineering" title="Bioengineering">bioengineer</a> bacteria for the production of therapeutic proteins, such as <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Growth_factor" title="Growth factor">growth factors</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">antibodies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of their importance for research in general, samples of bacterial strains are isolated and preserved in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_Resource_Centers" title="Biological Resource Centers">Biological Resource Centers</a>. This ensures the availability of the strain to scientists worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid14766539_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid14766539-241">[241]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_bacteriology">History of bacteriology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the history of microbiology, see <a href="/wiki/Microbiology" title="Microbiology">Microbiology</a>. For the history of bacterial classification, see <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_taxonomy" title="Bacterial taxonomy">Bacterial taxonomy</a>. For the natural history of Bacteria, see <a href="/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor" title="Last universal common ancestor">Last universal common ancestor</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anthonie_van_Leeuwenhoek_(1632-1723)._Natuurkundige_te_Delft_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-957.jpeg"><img alt="painting of Antonie van Leeuwenhoek, in robe and frilled shirt, with ink pen and paper" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2547" data-file-width="2148" decoding="async" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Anthonie_van_Leeuwenhoek_%281632-1723%29._Natuurkundige_te_Delft_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-957.jpeg/220px-Anthonie_van_Leeuwenhoek_%281632-1723%29._Natuurkundige_te_Delft_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-957.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Anthonie_van_Leeuwenhoek_%281632-1723%29._Natuurkundige_te_Delft_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-957.jpeg/330px-Anthonie_van_Leeuwenhoek_%281632-1723%29._Natuurkundige_te_Delft_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-957.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Anthonie_van_Leeuwenhoek_%281632-1723%29._Natuurkundige_te_Delft_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-957.jpeg/440px-Anthonie_van_Leeuwenhoek_%281632-1723%29._Natuurkundige_te_Delft_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-957.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anthonie_van_Leeuwenhoek_(1632-1723)._Natuurkundige_te_Delft_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-957.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Antonie_van_Leeuwenhoek" title="Antonie van Leeuwenhoek">Antonie van Leeuwenhoek</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/Microbiologist" title="Microbiologist">microbiologist</a> and the first person to observe bacteria using a <a href="/wiki/Microscope" title="Microscope">microscope</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Bacteria were first observed by the Dutch microscopist <a href="/wiki/Antonie_van_Leeuwenhoek" title="Antonie van Leeuwenhoek">Antonie van Leeuwenhoek</a> in 1676, using a single-lens <a href="/wiki/Microscope" title="Microscope">microscope</a> of his own design. He then published his observations in a series of letters to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_London" title="Royal Society of London">Royal Society of London</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWheelis2008_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWheelis2008-242">[242]</a></sup> Bacteria were Leeuwenhoek's most remarkable microscopic discovery. They were just at the limit of what his simple lenses could make out and, in one of the most striking hiatuses in the history of science, no one else would see them again for over a century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup> His observations had also included protozoans which he called <a href="/wiki/Animalcule" title="Animalcule">animalcules</a>, and his findings were looked at again in the light of the more recent findings of <a href="/wiki/Cell_theory" title="Cell theory">cell theory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPommerville20147_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPommerville20147-244">[244]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Christian_Gottfried_Ehrenberg" title="Christian Gottfried Ehrenberg">Christian Gottfried Ehrenberg</a> introduced the word "bacterium" in 1828.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup> In fact, his <i><a href="/wiki/Bacterium_(genus)" title="Bacterium (genus)">Bacterium</a></i> was a genus that contained non-spore-forming rod-shaped bacteria,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-status_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-status-246">[246]</a></sup> as opposed to <i>Bacillus</i>, a genus of spore-forming rod-shaped bacteria defined by Ehrenberg in 1835.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a> demonstrated in 1859 that the growth of microorganisms causes the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(food)" title="Fermentation (food)">fermentation</a> process, and that this growth is not due to <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_generation" title="Spontaneous generation">spontaneous generation</a> (<a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeasts</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mold_(fungus)" title="Mold (fungus)">molds</a>, commonly associated with fermentation, are not bacteria, but rather <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a>). Along with his contemporary <a href="/wiki/Robert_Koch" title="Robert Koch">Robert Koch</a>, Pasteur was an early advocate of the <a href="/wiki/Germ_theory_of_disease" title="Germ theory of disease">germ theory of disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup> Before them, <a href="/wiki/Ignaz_Semmelweis" title="Ignaz Semmelweis">Ignaz Semmelweis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Lister" title="Joseph Lister">Joseph Lister</a> had realised the importance of sanitized hands in medical work. Semmelweis ideas was rejected and his book on the topic condemned by the medical community, but after Lister doctors started sanitizing their hands in the 1870s. While Semmelweis who started with rules about handwashing in his hospital in the 1840s predated the spread of the ideas about germs themselves and attributed diseases to "decomposing animal organic matter", Lister was active later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup>
</p><p>Robert Koch, a pioneer in medical microbiology, worked on <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anthrax" title="Anthrax">anthrax</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>. In his research into tuberculosis Koch finally proved the germ theory, for which he received a <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">Nobel Prize</a> in 1905.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Koch%27s_postulates" title="Koch's postulates">Koch's postulates</a>, he set out criteria to test if an organism is the cause of a <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">disease</a>, and these postulates are still used today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Cohn" title="Ferdinand Cohn">Ferdinand Cohn</a> is said to be a founder of bacteriology, studying bacteria from 1870. Cohn was the first to classify bacteria based on their morphology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chung_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chung-252">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-microbeworld1_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-microbeworld1-253">[253]</a></sup>
</p><p>Though it was known in the nineteenth century that bacteria are the cause of many diseases, no effective <a href="/wiki/Antiseptic" title="Antiseptic">antibacterial</a> treatments were available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup> In 1910, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Ehrlich" title="Paul Ehrlich">Paul Ehrlich</a> developed the first antibiotic, by changing dyes that selectively stained <i><a href="/wiki/Treponema_pallidum" title="Treponema pallidum">Treponema pallidum</a></i>—the <a href="/wiki/Spirochaete" title="Spirochaete">spirochaete</a> that causes <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">syphilis</a>—into compounds that selectively killed the pathogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup> Ehrlich had been awarded a 1908 Nobel Prize for his work on <a href="/wiki/Immunology" title="Immunology">immunology</a>, and pioneered the use of stains to detect and identify bacteria, with his work being the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Gram_stain" title="Gram stain">Gram stain</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ziehl%E2%80%93Neelsen_stain" title="Ziehl–Neelsen stain">Ziehl–Neelsen stain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup>
</p><p>A major step forward in the study of bacteria came in 1977 when <a href="/wiki/Carl_Woese" title="Carl Woese">Carl Woese</a> recognised that archaea have a separate line of evolutionary descent from bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woese1977_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-Woese1977-257">[257]</a></sup> This new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogenetic" title="Phylogenetic">phylogenetic</a> <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomy</a> depended on the <a href="/wiki/Sequencing" title="Sequencing">sequencing</a> of <a href="/wiki/16S_ribosomal_RNA" title="16S ribosomal RNA">16S ribosomal RNA</a>, and divided prokaryotes into two evolutionary domains, as part of the <a href="/wiki/Three-domain_system" title="Three-domain system">three-domain system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHall2008145_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHall2008145-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
